	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hindu calendar</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">calendars used by Hindus on the Indian subcontinent</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg/220px-Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="595" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg/330px-Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg/440px-Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="1731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_calendar_1871-72.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the Hindu calendar 1871-72</div></div></div>
<p><b>Hindu calendar</b> is a collective term for the various <a href="/wiki/Lunisolar_calendar" title="Lunisolar calendar">lunisolar calendars</a> traditionally used in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. They adopt a similar underlying concept for timekeeping, but differ in their relative emphasis to moon cycle or the sun cycle and the names of months and when they consider the New Year to start.<sup id="cite_ref-richmond80_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richmond80-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Of the various regional calendars, the most studied and known Hindu calendars are the <a href="/wiki/Shaka_era" title="Shaka era">Shalivahana Shaka</a> found in South India, <a href="/wiki/Vikram_Samvat" title="Vikram Samvat">Vikram Samvat</a> (Bikrami) found in North and Central regions of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_calendar" title="Tamil calendar">Tamil calendar</a> used in Tamil Nadu, and the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali calendar">Bengali calendar</a> used in the Bengal – all of which emphasize the lunar cycle. Their new year starts in spring. In contrast, in regions such as Kerala, the solar cycle is emphasized and this is called the <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_calendar" title="Malayalam calendar">Malayalam calendar</a>, their new year starts in autumn, and these have origins in the second half of the 1st millennium CE.<sup id="cite_ref-richmond80_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richmond80-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2004p109_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2004p109-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A Hindu calendar is sometimes referred to as <b>Panchanga</b> (पञ्चाङ्ग).<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Hindu calendar conceptual design is also found in the Jewish calendar, but different from the Gregorian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Unlike Gregorian calendar which adds additional days to lunar month to adjust for the mismatch between twelve lunar cycles (354 lunar days)<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and nearly 365 solar days, the Hindu calendar maintains the integrity of the lunar month, but insert an extra full month by complex rules, every few years, to ensure that the festivals and crop-related rituals fall in the appropriate season.<sup id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2004p109_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2004p109-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hindu calendars have been in use in the Indian subcontinent since ancient times, and remains in use by the <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> in India and Nepal particularly to set the Hindu festival dates such as <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saraswati_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Saraswati Puja">Saraswati Puja</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Maha_Shivaratri" title="Maha Shivaratri">Maha Shivaratri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rath_Yatra" class="mw-redirect" title="Rath Yatra">Rath Yatra</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Navratri" class="mw-redirect" title="Navratri">Navratri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raksha_Bandhan" title="Raksha Bandhan">Raksha Bandhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganesh_Puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganesh Puja">Ganesh Puja</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Pongal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pongal">Pongal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onam" title="Onam">Onam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krishna_Janmashtami" title="Krishna Janmashtami">Krishna Janmashtami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durga_Puja" title="Durga Puja">Durga Puja</a>, Laxmi Puja, <a href="/wiki/Ram_Navami" class="mw-redirect" title="Ram Navami">Ram Navami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pana_Sankranti" title="Pana Sankranti">Pana Sankranti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vishu" title="Vishu">Vishu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>. Early Buddhist communities of India adopted the ancient Indian calendar, later Vikrami calendar and then local <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_calendar" title="Buddhist calendar">Buddhist calendars</a>. Buddhist festivals continue to be scheduled according to a lunar system.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_calendar" title="Buddhist calendar">Buddhist calendar</a> and the traditional lunisolar calendars of <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> are also based on an older version of the Hindu calendar. Similarly, the ancient <a href="/wiki/Jain" class="mw-redirect" title="Jain">Jain</a> traditions have followed the same lunisolar system as the Hindu calendar for festivals, texts and inscriptions. However, the Buddhist and Jain timekeeping systems have attempted to use the Buddha and the Mahavira's lifetimes as their reference points.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hindu calendar is also important to the practice of Hindu astrology and zodiac system.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="; background-color: #FFE0BB;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><b>Time keeping</b>
<div class="poem">
<p>[The current year] minus one,<br />
multiplied by twelve,<br />
multiplied by two,<br />
added to the elapsed [half months of current year],<br />
increased by two for every sixty [in the sun],<br />
is the quantity of half-months (<a href="/wiki/Syzygy_(astronomy)" title="Syzygy (astronomy)">syzygies</a>).
</p>
</div></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Rigveda Jyotisha-vedanga 4<br />Translator: Kim Plofker<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200936_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200936-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Time keeping was important to Vedic rituals, and <i>Jyotisha</i> was the Vedic era field of tracking and predicting the movements of astronomical bodies in order to keep time, in order to fix the day and time of these rituals.<sup id="cite_ref-monierwilliamsnijyotisa_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monierwilliamsnijyotisa-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jameslochtefeldsca326_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jameslochtefeldsca326-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mullerhaslp210_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mullerhaslp210-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This study was one of the six ancient <a href="/wiki/Vedanga" title="Vedanga">Vedangas</a>, or ancillary science connected with the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> – the scriptures of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vedic_Sanatan_Sanskriti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vedic Sanatan Sanskriti (page does not exist)">Vedic Sanatan Sanskriti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-monierwilliamsnijyotisa_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monierwilliamsnijyotisa-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jameslochtefeldsca326_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jameslochtefeldsca326-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The ancient Indian culture developed a sophisticated time keeping methodology and calendars for Vedic rituals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200910,_35–36,_67_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200910,_35–36,_67-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>David Pingree has proposed that the field of timekeeping in Jyotisha may have been "derived from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> period",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPingree19731–12_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPingree19731–12-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> but Yukio Ohashi considers this proposal as "definitely wrong".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Ohashi states that this Vedanga field developed from actual astronomical studies in ancient India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719–721_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719–721-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The texts of Vedic Jyotisha sciences were translated into the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese language</a> in the 2nd and 3rd centuries CE, and the Rigvedic passages on astronomy are found in the works of Zhu Jiangyan and <a href="/wiki/Zhi_Qian" title="Zhi Qian">Zhi Qian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPingree19732_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPingree19732-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Timekeeping as well as the nature of solar and moon movements are mentioned in Vedic texts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1993185–251_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1993185–251-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> For example, Kaushitaki <a href="/wiki/Brahmana" title="Brahmana">Brahmana</a> chapter 19.3 mentions the shift in the relative location of the sun towards north for 6 months, and south for 6 months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999720_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999720-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200935–42_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker200935–42-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Vikrami calendar is named after king <a href="/wiki/Vikramaditya" title="Vikramaditya">Vikramaditya</a> and starts in 57 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Texts">Texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dharmic (Hindu) scholars kept precise time by observing and calculating the cycles of (<a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a>) i.e. the sun, moon and the planets. These calculations about the sun appear in various astronomical texts in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, such as the 5th-century <i>Aryabhatiya</i> by <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>, the 6th-century <i>Romaka</i> by Latadeva and <i>Panca Siddhantika</i> by Varahamihira, the 7th-century <i>Khandakhadyaka</i> by Brahmagupta and the 8th-century <i>Sisyadhivrddida</i> by Lalla.<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess1989vii_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1989vii-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> These texts present Surya and various planets and estimate the characteristics of the respective planetary motion.<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess1989vii_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1989vii-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Other texts such as <i>Surya Siddhanta</i> dated to have been completed sometime between the 5th century and 10th century present their chapters on various deified planets with stories behind them.<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess1989vii_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1989vii-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The manuscripts of these texts exist in slightly different versions. They present Surya, planet-based calculations and Surya's relative motion to earth. These vary in their data, suggesting that the text were open and revised over their lives.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> For example, the 1st millennium CE Sanathana Dharma (Hindu) scholars calculated the sidereal length of a year as follows, from their astronomical studies, with slightly different results:<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess198926_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess198926-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" align="center" style="background: transparent;">
<caption>Smskrth (Sanskrit) texts: How many days in a year?
</caption>
<tbody><tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><b>Hindu text</b>
</td>
<td><b>Estimated length of the <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_year" title="Sidereal year">sidereal year</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-Burgess198926_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess198926-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Surya_Siddhanta" title="Surya Siddhanta">Surya Siddhanta</a></i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 36.56 seconds
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i>Paulica Siddhanta</i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 36 seconds
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i>Paracara Siddhanta</i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 31.50 seconds
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i>Arya Siddhanta</i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 30.84 seconds
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i>Laghu Arya Siddhanta</i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 30 seconds
</td></tr>
<tr style="text-align: center;">
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Siddhanta_Shiromani" class="mw-redirect" title="Siddhanta Shiromani">Siddhanta Shiromani</a></i>
</td>
<td>365 days, 6 hours, 12 minutes, 9 seconds
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Hindu texts used the lunar cycle for setting months and days, but the solar cycle to set the complete year. This system is similar to the Jewish and Babylonian ancient calendars, creating the same challenge of accounting for mismatch between the nearly 354 lunar days in twelve months, versus nearly 365 solar days in a year.<sup id="cite_ref-nesbittbc_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nesbittbc-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> They tracked the solar year by observing the entrance and departure of <i>surya</i> (sun, at sunrise and sunset) in the constellation formed by stars in the sky, which they divided into 12 intervals of 30 degrees each.<sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Like other ancient human cultures, Hindus innovated a number of systems of which intercalary months became most used, that is adding another month every 32.5 months on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> As their calendar keeping and astronomical observations became more sophisticated, the Hindu calendar became more sophisticated with complex rules and greater accuracy.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fuller292_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuller292-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Scott Montgomery, the <i>siddhanta</i> tradition at the foundation of Hindu calendars predate the Christian era, once had 18 texts of which only 5 have survived into the modern era.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> These texts provide specific information and formulae on motions of sun, moon and planets, to predict their future relative positions, equinoxes, rise and set, with corrections for prograde, retrograde motions, as well as parallax. These ancient scholars attempted to calculate their time to the accuracy of a <i>truti</i> (29.63 microseconds). In their pursuit of accurate tracking of relative movements of celestial bodies for their calendar, they had computed the mean diameter of earth, which was very close to the actual 12,742 km (7,918 mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindu calendars were refined during the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Gupta period">Gupta era</a> astronomy by <a href="/wiki/%C4%80ryabha%E1%B9%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Āryabhaṭa">Āryabhaṭa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varahamihira" class="mw-redirect" title="Varahamihira">Varāhamihira</a> in the 5th to 6th century. These in turn were based in the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu astronomy">astronomical tradition</a> of  <i><a href="/wiki/Vedanga_Jyotisha" title="Vedanga Jyotisha">Vedāṅga Jyotiṣa</a></i>, which in the preceding centuries had been standardised in a number of (non-extant) works known as  <i><a href="/wiki/Surya_Siddhanta" title="Surya Siddhanta">Sūrya Siddhānta</a></i>. Regional diversification took place in the medieval period. The astronomical foundations were further developed in the medieval period, notably by <a href="/wiki/Bh%C4%81skara_II" title="Bhāskara II">Bhāskara II</a> (12th century).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astrology">Astrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Astrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Later, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Jyotisha" title="Jyotisha">Jyotisha</a></i> evolved to include <a href="/wiki/Hindu_astrology" title="Hindu astrology">Hindu astrology</a>. The astrological application of the Hindu calendar was a field that likely developed in the centuries after the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_astrology" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek astrology">astrology</a> with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719–721_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYukio_Ohashi1999719–721-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPingree19732–3_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPingree19732–3-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> because their zodiac signs are nearly identical.<sup id="cite_ref-jameslochtefeldsca326_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jameslochtefeldsca326-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Hindu texts on Jyotisha only discuss time keeping, and never mention astrology or prophecy.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> These ancient texts predominantly cover astronomy, but at a rudimentary level.<sup id="cite_ref-mullerhaslp210_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mullerhaslp210-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Technical horoscopes and astrology ideas in India came from Greece, states European specialist Nicholas Campion, and developed in the early centuries of the 1st millennium CE.<sup id="cite_ref-campion110_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campion110-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Later medieval era texts such as the <i>Yavana-jataka</i> and the <i>Siddhanta</i> texts are more astrology-related.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker2009116–120,_259–261_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim_Plofker2009116–120,_259–261-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Balinese_Hindu_calendar">Balinese Hindu calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Balinese Hindu calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hinduism and Buddhism were the prominent religions of southeast Asia in the 1st millennium CE, prior to the Islamic conquest that started in the 14th century. The Hindus prevailed in Bali, Indonesia and they have two types of Hindu calendar. One is a 210-day based <a href="/wiki/Pawukon_calendar" title="Pawukon calendar">Pawukon calendar</a> which likely is a pre-Hindu system, and another is similar to lunisolar calendar system found in South India and it is called the <a href="/wiki/Balinese_saka_calendar" title="Balinese saka calendar">Balinese saka calendar</a> which uses Hindu methodology.<sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p153_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p153-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The names of month and festivals of Balinese Hindus for the most part are different, though the significance and legends have some overlap.<sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p153_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p153-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Year:_Samvat">Year: Samvat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Year: Samvat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Samvat</i> refers to era of the several Hindu calendar systems in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, in a manner that the consecutive years 1 BC and AD 1 mark the <a href="/wiki/Christian_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian era">Christian era</a> and the BC/AD system. There are several <i>samvat</i> found in historic Buddhist, Hindu and Jaina texts and epigraphy, of which three are most significant: Vikrama era, Old Shaka era and Shaka era of 78 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Salomon1998p181_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomon1998p181-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KedukanBukit001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KedukanBukit001.jpg/180px-KedukanBukit001.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KedukanBukit001.jpg/270px-KedukanBukit001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/KedukanBukit001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KedukanBukit001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hindu calendar saka samvat system is found in Indonesian inscriptions, such as the above dated to 611 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vikram_Samvat" title="Vikram Samvat">Vikram Samvat</a>: A northern India almanac which started in 57 BCE, and is also called the <i>Vikrama Era</i>. It is related to the <a href="/wiki/Bikrami_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Bikrami calendar">Bikrami calendar</a>, and is linked to Vikramaditya. This system is common in epigraphic evidence from northern, western, central and eastern Indian subcontinent, particularly after the early centuries of the 1st millennium CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Salomon1998p181_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomon1998p181-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shaka_Samvat" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaka Samvat">Shaka Samvat</a>: There are two Shaka era system in scholarly use, one is called <i>Old Shaka Era</i>, whose epoch is uncertain, probably sometime in the 1st millennium BCE because ancient Buddhist, Jaina and Hindu inscriptions and texts use it. However, the starting point of Old Shaka Era is a subject of dispute among scholars. The second system is called <i>Saka Era of 78 AD</i>, or simply <i>Saka Era</i>, a system that is common in epigraphic evidence from southern India.<sup id="cite_ref-Salomon1998p181_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salomon1998p181-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Saka era of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>: The Hindu calendar system in Indonesia is attributed to the legend of Hindus arriving with a sage Aji Saka in the 1st century <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, in the March of 78 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Melton2011p652_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2011p652-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Numerous ancient and medieval era texts and inscriptions found in Indonesian islands use this reference year.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Casparis1978p15_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Casparis1978p15-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In mainland southeast Asia, the earliest verifiable use of Hindu Saka methodology in inscriptions is marked <i>Saka 533</i> in <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Borei_and_Phnom_Da" title="Angkor Borei and Phnom Da">Ankor Borei</a>, which corresponds to 611 CE, while in the Indonesian islands, the <a href="/wiki/Kedukan_Bukit_inscription" title="Kedukan Bukit inscription">Kedukan Bukti</a> inscription in Sumatra, marked to <i>Saka 605</i> (683 CE) is one of the earliest known.<sup id="cite_ref-Casparis1978p15_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Casparis1978p15-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> However, these inscriptions set the <a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">floruit</a>, and the Hindu calendaring methodology likely existed in southeast Asia before these dates to be used in important monuments. Further, the Hindu calendar system remained popular among the Hindus through about the 15th century, and thereafter in Bali Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-Casparis1978p15_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Casparis1978p15-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indian_national_calendar" title="Indian national calendar">Indian national calendar</a> (modern): combined many Hindu calendars into one official standardized one, but old ones remain in use.<sup id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2007p490_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2007p490-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Months">Months</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Months">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span id="Amanta.2C_Purnimanta_systems"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Amanta,_Purnimanta_systems">Amanta, Purnimanta systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Amanta, Purnimanta systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two traditions have been followed in the Indian subcontinent with respect to lunar months: Amanta tradition which ends the lunar month on <a href="/wiki/Dark_moon" title="Dark moon">no moon</a> day, while Purnimanta tradition which ends it on <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full moon</a> day.<sup id="cite_ref-Dikshitar1993p24_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikshitar1993p24-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amavasyant (Amanta, Mukhyamana) tradition is followed by all Indian states that have a peninsular coastline (except Odisha), as well as Assam and Tripura.The states are Gujarat,Maharashtra,Karnataka,Kerala,Tamilnadu,Andhra pradesh,Telangana, and West Bengal.Odisha and all other states follow the Purnimanta (Gaunamana) tradition.
</p><p>Purnimanta tradition was being followed in the Vedic era. It was replaced with Amanta system and in use as the Hindu calendar system prior to 1st century BCE, but the Purnimanta tradition was restarted in 57 BCE by Vikramaditya who wanted to return to the Vedic roots.<sup id="cite_ref-Dikshitar1993p24_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikshitar1993p24-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The presence of this system is one of the factors considered in dating ancient manuscripts and epigraphical evidence of India that have survived into the modern era.<sup id="cite_ref-Dikshitar1993p24_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dikshitar1993p24-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paksha">Paksha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Paksha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A month contains two fortnights called <i><a href="/wiki/Paksha" title="Paksha">pakṣa</a></i> (पक्ष, literally "side").<sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2004p109_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2004p109-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> One fortnight is the bright, waxing half where the moon size grows and it ends in the full moon. This is called <i>Shukla Paksha</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The other half is the darkening, waning fortnight which ends in the new moon.<sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2004p109_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2004p109-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The Hindu festivals typically are either on or the day after the full moon night, or the darkest night (<i>amavasya</i>, अमावास्या), except for some associated with <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a>. The lunar months of the hot summer and the busy major cropping-related part of the monsoon season typically do not schedule major festivals.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A combination of the Paksha system, and the two traditions of Amanta and Purnimanta systems, has led to alternate ways of dating any festival or event in the historic Hindu, Buddhist or Jain literature, and contemporary regional literature or festival calendars. For example, the Hindu festival of colors called <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a> falls on the first day (full moon) of <i>Chaitra</i> lunar month's dark fortnight in the Purnimanta system, while the same exact day for Holi is expressed in Amanta system as the <i>Purnima</i> (full moon) lunar day of <i>Phalguna</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fuller292_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuller292-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Both time measuring and dating systems are equivalent ways of meaning the same thing, they continue to be in use in different regions, though the Purnimanta system is now typically assumed as implied in modern Indology literature if not specified.<sup id="cite_ref-fuller292_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fuller292-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_Month_names">Solar Month names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Solar Month names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are 12 months in the Vedic lunar calendar (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa"><a href="https://sa.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E0%A4%AE%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%B8%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%83" class="extiw" title="sa:मासाः">मासाः</a></span>). If the transits of the Sun through various constellations (Rāśi) are used, then we get solar months, which do not shift with reference to the Gregorian calendar. In practice, solar months are mostly referred as Rāśi (not months). The solar months (Rāśi) along with the corresponding Hindu seasons and Gregorian months are:<sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_Months_and_approximate_correspondence">Lunar Months and approximate correspondence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lunar Months and approximate correspondence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The names of the Indian months vary by region. Despite the significant differences between Indo-European languages and Dravidian languages, those Hindu calendars which are based on lunar cycle are generally phonetic variants of each other, while the solar cycle are generally variants of each other too, suggesting that the time keeping knowledge travelled widely across the Indian subcontinent in ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-richmond80_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-richmond80-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Tamil lunar month names are forward shifted by a month compared to Vikrami month names, in part because Tamil calendar integrates greater emphasis for the solar cycle in a manner similar to the neighboring Kerala region and it follows the Amanta system for lunar months. This is in contrast to Vikrami calendar which keeps the Purnimanta system and emphasizes the lunar cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A few major calendars are summarized below:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corrections_between_lunar_and_solar_months">Corrections between lunar and solar months</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Corrections between lunar and solar months">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:854px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_3&quot; Also illustrates Adhika Masa (Year 2-Bhadrapada) repeats; the first time the Sun moves entirely within Simha Rashi thus rendering it an Adhika Masa</div></div></div>
<p>Twelve Hindu mas (māsa, lunar month) are equal to approximately 354 days, while the length of a sidereal (solar) year is about 365 days. This creates a difference of about eleven days, which is offset every (29.53/10.63) = 2.71 years, or approximately every 32.5 months.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The twelve months are subdivided into six lunar seasons timed with the agriculture cycles, blooming of natural flowers, fall of leaves, and weather. To account for the mismatch between lunar and solar calendar, the Hindu scholars adopted intercalary months, where a particular month just repeated. The choice of this month was not random, but timed to sync back the two calendars to the cycle of agriculture and nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery2015p103_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery2015p103-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reingold2008p275_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reingold2008p275-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The repetition of a month created the problem of scheduling festivals, weddings and other social events without repetition and confusion. This was resolved by declaring one month as <i>Shudha</i> (pure, clean, regular, proper, also called <i>Deva</i> month) and the other <i>Mala</i> or <i>Adhika</i> (extra, unclean and inauspicious, also called <a href="/wiki/Purushottam_Maas" class="mw-redirect" title="Purushottam Maas">Asura masa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Underhill1991p32_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Underhill1991p32-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian mathematicians who calculated the best way to adjust the two years, over long periods of a <i>yuga</i> (era, tables calculating 1000 of years), they determined that the best means to intercalate the months is to time the intercalary months on a 19-year cycle. This intercalation is generally adopted in the 3rd, 5th, 8th, 11th, 14th, 16th and 19th year of this cycle. Further, the complex rules rule out the repetition of <a href="/wiki/Agrahayana" title="Agrahayana">Mārgasirsa</a> (also called <i>Agahana</i>), <a href="/wiki/Pausha" title="Pausha">Pausha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maagha" class="mw-redirect" title="Maagha">Maagha</a> lunar months. The historic Hindu texts are not consistent on these rules, with competing ideas flourishing in the Hindu culture.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rare_corrections">Rare corrections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rare corrections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Hindu calendar makes further rare adjustments, over a cycle of centuries, where a certain month is considered <i>kshaya</i> month (dropped). This occurs because of the complexity of the relative lunar, solar and earth movements. According to the Hindu calendar theory, states uriel Marion Underhill, "when the sun is in perigee, and a lunar month being at its longest, if the new moon immediately precedes a <a href="/wiki/Sankranti" title="Sankranti">samkranti</a>, then the first of the two lunar months is deleted (called <i>nija</i> or <i>kshaya</i>). This, for example, happened in the year 1 BCE, when there was no new moon between Makara samkranti and Kumbha samkranti, and the month of Pausha was dropped.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Day">Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Just like months, the Hindu calendar has two measures of a day, one based on the lunar movement and the other on solar. The solar day or civil day, called <i>divasa</i> (	दिवस), has been what most Hindus traditionally use, is easy and empirical to observe, by poor and rich, with or without a clock, and it is defined as the period from one sunrise to another. The lunar day is called <i>tithi</i> (तिथि), and this is based on complicated measures of lunar movement. A lunar day or <i>tithi</i> may, for example, begin in the middle of an afternoon and end next afternoon.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Both these days do not directly correspond to a mathematical measure for a day such as equal 24 hours of a solar year, a fact that the Hindu calendar scholars knew, but the system of <i>divasa</i> was convenient for the general population. The <i>tithi</i> have been the basis for timing rituals and festivals, while <i>divasa</i> for everyday use. The Hindu calendars adjust the mismatch in <i>divasa</i> and <i>tithi</i>, using a methodology similar to the solar and lunar months.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>Tithi</i> is technically defined in Indian texts, states <a href="/wiki/John_E._Cort" title="John E. Cort">John E. Cort</a>, as "the time required by the combined motions of the sun and moon to increase (in a bright fortnight) or decrease (in a dark fortnight) their relative distance by twelve degrees of the zodiac.<sup id="cite_ref-Cort2001p2_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cort2001p2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> These motions are measured using a fixed map of celestial zodiac as reference, and given the elliptical orbits, a duration of a <i>tithi</i> varies between 21.5 and 26 hours, states Cort.<sup id="cite_ref-Cort2001p2_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cort2001p2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> However, in the Indian tradition, the general population's practice has been to treat a tithi as a solar day between one sunrise to next.<sup id="cite_ref-Cort2001p2_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cort2001p2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A lunar month has 30 <i>tithi</i>. The technical standard makes each <i>tithi</i> contain different number of hours, but helps the overall integrity of the calendar. Given the variation in the length of a solar day with seasons, and moon's relative movements, the start and end time for <i>tithi</i> varies over the seasons and over the years, and the <i>tithi</i> adjusted to sync with <i>divasa</i> periodically with intercalation.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Weekday.2FV.C4.81sara"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Weekday/Vāsara">Weekday/Vāsara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Weekday/Vāsara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Vāsara</i> refers to the weekdays in Sanskrit.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Also referred to as <i>Vara</i> and used as a suffix.<sup id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2007p490_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2007p490-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The correspondence between the <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_days_of_the_week" title="Names of the days of the week">names of the week</a> in Hindu and other Indo-European calendars are exact. This alignment of names probably took place sometime during the 3rd century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-underhill25_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-underhill25-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dalal2010p89_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalal2010p89-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The weekday of a Hindu calendar has been symmetrically divided into 60 <i>ghatika</i> (= 24 hours), each <i>ghatika</i> is divided into 60 <i>pala</i> (= 24 minutes), each <i>pala</i> is subdivided into 60 <i>vipala</i> (= 24 seconds), and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-underhill25_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-underhill25-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td>Baţėvār<br />बटु'वार <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;"> بٹ وار</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Punjabi Muslims use Urdu/Arabic words for Friday / Saturday etc.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<p>The term <b>-vāsara</b> is often realised as <b>vāra</b> or <b>vaar</b> in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>-derived and influenced languages. There are many variations of the names in the regional languages, mostly using alternate names of the celestial bodies involved.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_limbs_of_time">Five limbs of time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Five limbs of time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The complete Indian calendars contain five <i>angas</i> or parts of information: lunar day (tithi), solar day (diwas), <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterism</a> (naksatra), planetary joining (yoga) and astronomical period (karanam). This structure gives the calendar the name <i>Panchangam</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2007p490_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2007p490-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The first two are discussed above.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yoga">Yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Hindu+calendar%22">"Hindu calendar"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Hindu+calendar%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Hindu+calendar%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Hindu+calendar%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Hindu+calendar%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Hindu+calendar%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2010</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word Yoga means "union, joining, attachment", but in astronomical context, this word means latitudinal and longitudinal information. The longitude of the sun and the longitude of the moon are added, and normalised to a value ranging between 0° to 360° (if greater than 360, one subtracts 360). This sum is divided into 27 parts. Each part will now equal 800' (where ' is the symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Arcminute" class="mw-redirect" title="Arcminute">arcminute</a> which means 1/60 of a degree). These parts are called the <i>yogas</i>. They are labelled:
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">
<ol><li>Viṣkambha</li>
<li>Prīti</li>
<li>Āyuśmān</li>
<li>Saubhāgya</li>
<li>Śobhana</li>
<li>Atigaṇḍa</li>
<li>Sukarma</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dhrti" title="Dhrti">Dhrti</a></li>
<li>Śūla</li>
<li>Gaṇḍa</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/V%E1%B9%9Bddhi" title="Vṛddhi">Vṛddhi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dhruva" title="Dhruva">Dhruva</a></li>
<li>Vyāghatā</li>
<li>Harṣaṇa</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vajra" title="Vajra">Vajra</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siddhi" title="Siddhi">Siddhi</a></li>
<li>Vyatipāta</li>
<li>Variyas</li>
<li>Parigha</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aiva" class="mw-redirect" title="Śiva">Śiva</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siddha" title="Siddha">Siddha</a></li>
<li>Sādhya</li>
<li>Śubha</li>
<li>Śukla</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a></li>
<li>Māhendra</li>
<li>Vaidhṛti</li></ol>
</div>
<p>Again, minor variations may exist. The <i>yoga</i> that is active during sunrise of a day is the prevailing <i>yoga</i> for the day.
</p>
<h4><span id="Kara.E1.B9.87a"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Karaṇa">Karaṇa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Karaṇa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A <b>karaṇa</b> is half of a <b><a href="/wiki/Tithi" title="Tithi">tithi</a></b>. To be precise, a karaṇa is the time required for the angular distance between the sun and the moon to increase in steps of 6° starting from 0°. (Compare with the definition of a tithi.)
</p><p>Since the tithis are 30 in number, and since 1 tithi = 2 karaṇas, therefore one would logically expect there to be 60 karaṇas. But there are only 11 such karaṇas which fill up those slots to accommodate for those 30 tithis. There are actually 4 "fixed" (sthira) karaṇas and 7 "repeating" (cara) karaṇas.
</p><p>The 4
</p>
<ol><li>Śakuni (शकुनि)</li>
<li>Catuṣpāda (चतुष्पाद)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/N%C4%81ga" title="Nāga">Nāga</a> (नाग)</li>
<li>Kiṃstughna(किंस्तुघ्न)</li></ol>
<p>The 7 "repeating" karaṇas are:<sup id="cite_ref-Burgess1989_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1989-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Vava" class="mw-redirect" title="Vava">Vava</a> or Bava (बव)</li>
<li>Valava or Bālava (बालव)</li>
<li>Kaulava (कौलव)</li>
<li>Taitila or Taitula (तैतिल)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gara" title="Gara">Gara</a> or Garaja (गरज)</li>
<li>Vaṇija (वणिज)</li>
<li>Viṣṭi (<a href="/wiki/Bhadra_(Hindu_calendar)" title="Bhadra (Hindu calendar)">Bhadra</a>) (भद्रा)</li></ol>
<ul><li>Now the first half of the 1st <i>tithi</i> (of Śukla Pakṣa) is always <i>Kiṃtughna karaṇa</i>. Hence this <b>karaṇa</b> is "fixed".</li>
<li>Next, the 7-repeating karaṇas repeat eight times to cover the next 56 half-<i>tithis</i>. Thus these are the "repeating" (cara) karaṇas.</li>
<li>The 3 remaining half-<i>tithis</i> take the remaining "fixed" karaṇas in order. Thus these are also "fixed" (sthira).</li>
<li>Thus one gets 60 karaṇas from those 11 preset karaṇas.</li></ul>
<p>The Vedic day begins at sunrise. The karaṇa at sunrise of a particular day shall be the prevailing karaṇa for the whole day.
(citation needed )
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nakshatra">Nakshatra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nakshatra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nakshatras" class="mw-redirect" title="Nakshatras">Nakshatras</a> are divisions of ecliptic, each 13° 20', starting from 0° Aries. The purnima of each month is synchronised with a nakshatra.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Festival_calendar:_solar_and_lunar_dates">Festival calendar: solar and lunar dates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Festival calendar: solar and lunar dates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many holidays in the Hindu, Buddhist and Jaina traditions are based on the lunar cycles in the lunisolar timekeeping with foundations in the Hindu calendar system. A few holidays, however, are based on the solar cycle, such as the <a href="/wiki/Vaisakhi" title="Vaisakhi">Vaisakhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pongal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pongal">Pongal</a> and those associated with Sankranti.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The dates of the lunar cycle based festivals vary significantly on the Gregorian calendar and sometimes as much as weeks. The solar cycle based ancient Indian festivals almost always fall on the same Gregorian date every year and if they vary in an exceptional year, it is by one day.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variants">Regional variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regional variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Indian Calendar Reform Committee, appointed in 1952, identified more than thirty well-developed calendars, in use across different parts of India.<sup id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2007p490_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2007p490-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Variants include the lunar emphasizing <i>Vikrama</i>, the <i>Shalivahana</i> calendars, as well as the solar emphasizing <a href="/wiki/Tamil_calendar" title="Tamil calendar">Tamil calendar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_calendar" title="Malayalam calendar">Malayalam calendar</a>. The two calendars most widely used today are the <i><a href="/wiki/Vikram_Samvat" title="Vikram Samvat">Vikrama</a></i> calendar, which is in followed in western and <a href="/wiki/Northern_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern India">northern India</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Shalivahana_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Shalivahana era">Shalivahana Shaka</a>  calendar which is followed in the <a href="/wiki/Deccan" class="mw-redirect" title="Deccan">Deccan</a> region of India (Comprising present day Indian states of <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Underhill1921_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Underhill1921-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
